import math

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

# Default ModelNew is PyTorch-only. run_and_check: use check_kernel=False with backend=cuda
# until the candidate file contains a real CUDA kernel (see kernel_static_checker).


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Graph-Fused VLA (GF-VLA) style **scene graph relation update** in one forward.

    Objects are nodes with vision-language aligned embeddings; directed pairs
    (i → j) carry pairwise conditioning (geometry, language grounding, or fused
    VLA features). This reference fuses:

      LayerNorm(nodes) → multi-head attention over objects with **pairwise
      relation MLP bias** on edges → residual write-back.

    Intended as a fusion target for navigation / manipulation stacks that maintain
    explicit scene graphs and update them from multimodal observations.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, num_heads: int):
        super().__init__()
        assert dim % num_heads == 0
        self.dim = dim
        self.pair_dim = pair_dim
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = dim // num_heads
        self.scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(self.head_dim)

        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)
        self.q_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.k_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.v_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.rel_mlp = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim),
            nn.SiLU(),
            nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads),
        )
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)

    def forward(
        self,
        nodes: torch.Tensor,
        pair_feats: torch.Tensor,
        edge_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            nodes: (B, N, D) object / region embeddings.
            pair_feats: (B, N, N, P) pairwise VLA or geometric conditioning.
            edge_mask: (B, N, N) float in {0, 1}; 1 keeps attention over column j.
        Returns:
            (B, N, D) updated node embeddings (residual around attention block).
        """
        B, N, D = nodes.shape
        _, N2, N3, P = pair_feats.shape
        assert N == N2 == N3 and P == self.pair_dim
        h = self.norm(nodes)

        q = self.q_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)
        k = self.k_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)
        v = self.v_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)

        logits = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)) * self.scale
        rel_bias = self.rel_mlp(pair_feats).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)
        logits = logits + rel_bias

        logits = logits.masked_fill(edge_mask.unsqueeze(1) < 0.5, float("-inf"))
        attn = F.softmax(logits, dim=-1)
        ctx = torch.matmul(attn, v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, N, D)
        return nodes + self.out_proj(ctx)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    """KernelBench candidate entry point; default delegates to ``Model``."""

    def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, num_heads: int):
        super().__init__()
        self._impl = Model(dim, pair_dim, num_heads)

    def forward(
        self,
        nodes: torch.Tensor,
        pair_feats: torch.Tensor,
        edge_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        return self._impl(nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask)


batch_size = 4
num_objects = 24
dim = 96
pair_dim = 16
num_heads = 4


def get_inputs():
    nodes = torch.randn(batch_size, num_objects, dim)
    pair_feats = torch.randn(batch_size, num_objects, num_objects, pair_dim)
    edge_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, num_objects, num_objects)
    return [nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim, pair_dim, num_heads]

We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for this attention block. Let's analyze the model: - LayerNorm on nodes - Linear projections Q, K, V (no bias) - Dot-product attention scaled by 1/sqrt(head_dim) - Pairwise relation MLP bias: pair_feats (B, N, N, P) -> Linear(P,P) -> SiLU -> Linear(P, H) -> permute to (B, H, N, N) - Add to logits - Mask with edge_mask (B, N, N) unsqueezed to (B, 1, N, N): fill <0.5 with -inf - Softmax over last dimension - Multiply attn by V - Transpose and reshape to (B, N, D) - Linear out_proj (no bias) - Residual add Goal: custom kernel faster than PyTorch reference. Since D%32=0, bank = k%32. So for fixed k, all threads read the same bank. But they read different addresses: d0*D+k vs d1*D+k. The distance is multiple of D=96, which is multiple of 32. So they are in the same bank but different "rows"? Shared memory bank conflict means multiple threads accessing different addresses in the same bank. So yes, this is a 32-way bank conflict for reading s_w. Not good. How to avoid? We can pad the weight shared memory: s_w[d][D_padded]. If we set D_padded = D+1 = 97, then bank = (d*97 + k) % 32. For consecutive d, bank changes. Good. But we need to allocate accordingly. Alternatively, we can store the weight transposed but with padding to avoid conflicts. s_w[k][D_padded] where D_padded = D+1. Then for fixed k and varying d, address = k*(D+1) + d. Bank = (k*(D+1) + d) % 32. For consecutive d, consecutive banks. No conflict. And the writes during loading are also coalesced if we do it right. Let's do that: declare `float s_w[(D+1)*D];` Actually, if we store transposed, shape is D rows (k) by D columns (d). We can pad column dimension to D+1. So `s_w[k*(D+1) + d] = w[d*D + k]`. When loading from global to shared: for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < D*D; idx += blockDim.x) { int d = idx / D; int k = idx % D; s_w[k*(D+1) + d] = w[d*D + k]; } Now writes: for fixed d and varying k, threads write to s_w[k*(D+1)+d] which is strided by D+1=97. 97%32 = 1, so each thread writes to a different bank. Good. Reads: for fixed k and varying d, threads read s_w[k*(D+1)+d] which is contiguous (d varies), so fully coalesced. Perfect. Similarly, for s_nodes, we read s_nodes[n*D + k] where n is fixed and k varies. That's contiguous. If multiple threads have different n but same k (e.g., threads processing same n), that's broadcast. If threads processing different n, they read different rows, strided by D=96. Same bank conflict issue. But in our mapping, threads in the same warp have the same n? Let's check. In the QKV matmul, we process elements (n,d). With blockDim.x=256, threads are assigned via strided loop: thread t gets idx = t, t+256, ... For D=96, idx=0..95 correspond to n=0,d=0..95. So first 96 threads (3 warps) have n=0, d varying. Next 96 threads have n=1. Next 64 have n=2,d=0..63. So within a warp (32 threads), for the first warp (t=0..31), n=0, d=0..31. So they all read s_nodes[0*D + k] (same n), which is broadcast. Good. They read s_w[k*(D+1) + d] where d varies: coalesced. Excellent. For the second warp (t=32..63), n=0, d=32..63. Same s_nodes row, coalesced s_w. Good. Third warp: n=0, d=64..95. Good. Fourth warp: n=1, d=0..31. Same pattern. So within each warp, all threads have the same n. Thus s_nodes reads are broadcast (fast). s_w reads are coalesced (no bank conflict because of padding). This is ideal. Great. We'll use padded shared memory for weights. **Kernel 2 details:** We need to define the attention kernel. Grid: B blocks. Since head_dim=24, we do 24 iterations. In each iteration, each thread does one shared read, one multiply, then warp shuffle reduce (5 shuffles). Let's detail: // Each warp handles one query (h,i) int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; int total_queries = num_heads * N; for (int qid = warp_id; qid < total_queries; qid += num_warps) { int h = qid / N; int i = qid % N; int q_offset = ((b * num_heads + h) * N + i) * head_dim; // global memory offset for q // Actually we have q in shared memory already. smem_q layout: [num_heads][N][head_dim] int smem_q_base = (h * N + i) * head_dim; int smem_k_base = h * N * head_dim; int smem_v_base = h * N * head_dim; float max_logit = -1e30f; float dot = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { int j = lane; // compute dot product q_i . k_j float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { sum += smem_q[smem_q_base + d] * smem_k[smem_k_base + j * head_dim + d]; } // compute rel_bias via MLP // load pair_feats float bias = 0.0f; // MLP: W1 (P,P), b1 (P), SiLU, W2 (H,P), b2 (H) // We'll compute hidden = W1 * pf + b1 // Since P is small (e.g., 16), we can compute directly. // Let's load pf into registers: we can read from pair_feats global memory. int pf_base = ((b * N + i) * N + j) * pair_dim; // hidden[d] = sum_p W1[d][p] * pf[p] + b1[d] // Then bias = sum_d W2[h][d] * hidden[d] + b2[h] // We can compute without storing hidden array: // bias = W2[h] @ SiLU(W1 @ pf + b1) + b2[h] // Since P is small, we can compute `float hidden[64];` if we have compile-time max. // Or we can unroll the loops if pair_dim is passed as argument. // Given pair_dim is likely small, we can allocate a fixed-size array, e.g., MAX_P=64. // But to be generic and avoid large stack, let's compute on the fly. // Actually, we need the hidden vector to dot with W2. We can compute hidden[p] for each p and accumulate dot with W2[h][p] simultaneously? No, because SiLU is elementwise and W2 dot is after SiLU. So we need to compute hidden[p] first. // We can local array `float hidden[64];` with a compile-time max. Let's set a reasonable max like 64. // Or we can compute in a small loop and store in registers. The compiler will keep it in registers if small. // Let's assume pair_dim <= 64. We'll allocate `float hidden[64];` and only use pair_dim. for (int d = 0; d < pair_dim; ++d) { float val = b1[d]; for (int p = 0; p < pair_dim; ++p) { val += W1[d * pair_dim + p] * pair_feats[pf_base + p]; } val = val * sigmoidf(val); // SiLU(x) = x * sigmoid(x) hidden[d] = val; } bias = b2[h]; for (int d = 0; d < pair_dim; ++d) { bias += W2[h * pair_dim + d] * hidden[d]; } dot = sum * scale + bias; // mask float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N) + i) * N + j]; if (mask_val < 0.5f) dot = -1e30f; // -inf approx max_logit = dot; } // warp reduce max for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_logit, offset); if (other > max_logit) max_logit = other; } // compute exp and sum float exp_val = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { exp_val = expf(dot - max_logit); } float sum_exp = exp_val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_exp += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_exp, offset); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; float attn_weight = (lane < N) ? exp_val * inv_sum : 0.0f; // compute output for each d for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float val = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { val = attn_weight * smem_v[smem_v_base + lane * head_dim + d]; } // warp sum for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) { smem_ctx[i * D + h * head_dim + d] = val; } } } This is very clean. Let's adopt this: Kernel 2 shared memory: - smem_ctx: N*D floats (attention output) - smem_w: D*(D+1) floats (W_out transposed and padded) Dynamic size = (N*D + D*(D+1)) * sizeof(float). For D=96,N=24: (2304 + 9312)*4 = 46464 bytes. Fits. Actually, to avoid magic numbers, we can compute padded size at runtime: `size_t smem_size = (N * D + D * (D + 1)) * sizeof(float);` and launch with that. **Kernel 2 algorithm:** ```cuda __global__ void fused_attn_out_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* pair_feats, const float* edge_mask, const float* nodes_orig, const float* W1, const float* b1, const float* W2, const float* b2, const float* W_out, float* out, int B, int N, int D, int H, int P, float scale) { ``` Inside: int b = blockIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_ctx = smem; float* smem_w = smem + N * D; // compute attention int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; int head_dim = D / H; int total_queries = H * N; for (int qid = warp_id; qid < total_queries; qid += num_warps) { int h = qid / N; int i = qid % N; int q_base = ((b * H + h) * N + i) * head_dim; int k_base = ((b * H + h) * N) * head_dim; int v_base = ((b * H + h) * N) * head_dim; // preload pair_feat for this (i, lane)? float pf[64]; // compile-time max, use P actual int pf_base = ((b * N + i) * N + lane) * P; if (lane < N) { for (int p = 0; p < P; ++p) { pf[p] = pair_feats[pf_base + p]; } } float dot = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { int j = lane; // compute q_i dot k_j float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { sum += q[q_base + d] * k[k_base + j * head_dim + d]; } // compute rel_bias float bias = b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; ++p) { float tmp = b1[p]; for (int q_idx = 0; q_idx < P; ++q_idx) { tmp += W1[p * P + q_idx] * pf[q_idx]; } tmp = tmp / (1.0f + expf(-tmp)); // SiLU = x * sigmoid(x) bias += W2[h * P + p] * tmp; } dot = sum * scale + bias; float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N) + i) * N + j]; if (mask_val < 0.5f) dot = -1e30f; } // warp reduce max float max_logit = dot; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_logit, offset); if (other > max_logit) max_logit = other; } float exp_val = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { exp_val = expf(dot - max_logit); } float sum_exp = exp_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_exp += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_exp, offset); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; float attn_weight = (lane < N) ? exp_val * inv_sum : 0.0f; // compute ctx for each d for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float val = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { val = attn_weight * v[v_base + lane * head_dim + d]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) { smem_ctx[i * D + h * head_dim + d] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); // Load W_out into shared memory transposed (padded) // smem_w[k][d] = W_out[d][k] for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < D * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int d = idx / D; int k = idx % D; smem_w[k * (D + 1) + d] = W_out[d * D + k]; } __syncthreads(); // out_proj + residual int total_out = N * D; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_out; idx += blockDim.x) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { sum += smem_ctx[n * D + k] * smem_w[k * (D + 1) + d]; } float orig = nodes_orig[b * N * D + n * D + d]; out[b * N * D + n * D + d] = orig + sum; } ``` Wait, `W_out` in the model is `out_proj.weight` with shape (D,D). The operation is `ctx @ W_out.T`, so `out[n][d] = sum_k ctx[n][k] * W_out[d][k]`. Yes, this matches. **Potential issues:** - `pf[64]` is a stack array. With lane < N check, if lane >= N we don't load, but the array is still there. That's fine. - `qid` loop: warp_id from 0..7. For total_queries=96, iterations: qid = warp_id + 0*8, warp_id + 1*8, ... up to <96. For warp_id=0: qid=0,8,16,...,88 (12 iterations). Good. Each warp does 12 queries. Good occupancy? With 256 threads per block and 8 warps, each warp does 12 queries. Total warps across B=4 blocks = 32 warps. SM can run multiple blocks. - `__shfl_down_sync` mask: we can use 0xFFFFFFFF, but lanes >= N might have dot = -1e30f and exp_val = 0. They won't affect the max if there is at least one real value. But `__shfl_down_sync` requires active threads. Since all threads in the warp participate in the loop (no conditional around the shuffle), we are fine. The `if (lane < N)` only guards the computation but the shuffle is called by all threads in the warp. Wait, we have: float max_logit = dot; for (...) { other = __shfl_down_sync(...); max_logit = fmaxf(max_logit, other); } This is called by all threads in the warp. Good. But `dot` is 0 for lane >= N (because we didn't set it). Actually, we set `float dot = 0.0f;` and then `if (lane < N) { ... dot = ... }`. So for lane >= N, dot = 0. But max_logit should be influenced only by real lanes. Since real lanes have N=24, and there are 24 of them, and dot values can be negative or positive. If all real dots are negative (e.g., -10), the max among real lanes is -10. Lane 24 has dot=0, which would become the max! That's a bug. We need to initialize dot to a very negative number for all lanes, and set mask_val to 0 for inactive lanes. So: float dot = -1e30f; if (lane < N) { ... dot = sum * scale + bias; if (mask_val < 0.5f) dot = -1e30f; } Then max_logit = dot. For lane >= N, dot = -1e30f, so they won't affect max. Similarly for exp_val: `float exp_val = 0.0f; if (lane < N) exp_val = expf(dot - max_logit);` For inactive lanes, exp_val=0. Good. - `nodes_orig`: The original nodes tensor. We need to pass it to Kernel 2. In the reference, the residual is added to the output of out_proj. So we need the original nodes, not the normalized ones. In our Kernel 1, we write q,k,v to global memory. We can keep the original nodes in global memory and pass the pointer to Kernel 2. That's easy. - `q`, `k`, `v` buffers: We need temporary storage. We can allocate them in `forward()` using `torch.empty_like(nodes)` and pass to kernels. - `smem_ctx` writing: Only lane 0 writes to smem_ctx per query. Since there are 96 queries, lane 0 of each warp writes 24 times (for each d). That's 96*24 = 2304 writes total. Since multiple warps may write to the same bank? Each warp writes to different (i, h*head_dim+d) which are distinct. Good. - `__syncthreads()` before loading W_out: we need all warps to finish writing smem_ctx. Then load W_out. Then `__syncthreads()`. Then compute out. - Edge mask: The reference uses `edge_mask.unsqueeze(1) < 0.5` and fills with -inf. Our kernel uses `edge_mask[b,i,j] < 0.5`. That's correct. - `scale`: 1.0 / sqrt(head_dim). We can pass as float. **Kernel 1 finalization:** Let's write the LN+QKV kernel. ```cuda __global__ void fused_ln_qkv_kernel( const float* nodes, const float* gamma, const float* beta, const float* Wq, const float* Wk, const float* Wv, float* q, float* k, float* v, int B, int N, int D, float eps) { ``` Dynamic shared memory: s_nodes (N*D), s_w (D*(D+1)). Total = same as before? Actually D*(D+1) + N*D. For D=96: 9792+2304=12096 floats = 48.4KB? Wait, 12096*4 = 48384 bytes. Slightly over 48KB! For D=96,N=24: N*D=2304. D*(D+1)=9312. Sum=11616. 11616*4=46464. Under 48KB. Good. But wait: we need Wq, Wk, Wv. We can load them one at a time into s_w. Same s_w buffer. Steps: 1. Load nodes into s_nodes. 2. __syncthreads() 3. LN. 4. __syncthreads() 5. For each proj in {Wq, Wk, Wv}: a. Load weight into s_w transposed padded. b. __syncthreads() c. Compute output and write to global q/k/v. d. __syncthreads() LN details: we decided to use warps for nodes. But if N is small, we can just handle it. Let's write generic: int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; for (int n_start = 0; n_start < N; n_start += num_warps) { int node = n_start + warp_id; if (node < N) { // compute mean float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { sum += s_nodes[node * D + d]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); float mean = sum / D; float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { float diff = s_nodes[node * D + d] - mean; sq_sum += diff * diff; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq_sum, offset); float var = sq_sum / D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { float val = (s_nodes[node * D + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * gamma[d] + beta[d]; s_nodes[node * D + d] = val; } } } Wait, `gamma` and `beta` are in global memory. Accessing them by lane index. Since D=96, lane 0..31 read gamma[0..31], gamma[32..63], gamma[64..95] across the three iterations of the loop. That's coalesced? The loop `for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32)` means in the first iteration d=lane, second d=lane+32, third d=lane+64. So each thread reads gamma[lane], gamma[lane+32], gamma[lane+64]. Consecutive threads read consecutive elements: gamma[0], gamma[1], ... gamma[31]. That's coalesced. Good. However, `gamma` and `beta` are broadcast to all nodes. So they will be in L1 cache. **Projection computation:** For each weight matrix: ```cuda for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < N * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { sum += s_nodes[n * D + k] * s_w[k * (D + 1) + d]; } out_ptr[b * N * D + idx] = sum; } ``` Where out_ptr is q, k, or v. **PyTorch side:** In ModelNew.__init__, we need to create the same layers as Model? Let's outline ModelNew.forward: ```python def forward(self, nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask): B, N, D = nodes.shape H = self.num_heads P = self.pair_dim head_dim = D // H # Ensure contiguous float nodes_f = nodes.contiguous().float() pair_feats_f = pair_feats.contiguous().float() edge_mask_f = edge_mask.contiguous().float() # extract weights gamma = self.norm.weight.contiguous().float() beta = self.norm.bias.contiguous().float() Wq = self.q_proj.weight.contiguous().float() # (D,D) Wk = self.k_proj.weight.contiguous().float() Wv = self.v_proj.weight.contiguous().float() W_out = self.out_proj.weight.contiguous().float() # rel_mlp weights W1 = self.rel_mlp[0].weight.contiguous().float() # (P,P) b1 = self.rel_mlp[0].bias.contiguous().float() # (P,) W2 = self.rel_mlp[2].weight.contiguous().float() # (H,P) b2 = self.rel_mlp[2].bias.contiguous().float() # (H,) q = torch.empty_like(nodes_f) k = torch.empty_like(nodes_f) v = torch.empty_like(nodes_f) out = torch.empty_like(nodes_f) # Call CUDA wrappers # Need a module-level reference to the compiled extension. # We can store it as self._cpp or a global variable. ext.fused_ln_qkv(nodes_f, gamma, beta, Wq, Wk, Wv, q, k, v) ext.fused_attn_out(q, k, v, pair_feats_f, edge_mask_f, nodes_f, W1, b1, W2, b2, W_out, out, B, N, D, H, P, 1.0 / math.sqrt(head_dim)) return out ``` But the compiled extension functions need to accept the tensors. Since sizes are small, we don't need to use `__restrict__` but it's good. Also, for the MLP bias computation in the attention kernel, we need to be careful about reading W1, W2, b1, b2. In C/C++ (row-major), indexing is: `pair_feats[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + p]`. Yes. Edge mask: `edge_mask[((b * N) + i) * N + j]`. q, k, v shapes: we will store them as (B, N, D) in global memory. Wait, in PyTorch, `.view(B, N, H, head_dim).transpose(1,2)` gives (B,H,N,head_dim). In memory, this is a contiguous tensor if we use `.contiguous()` after transpose. But we don't need to transpose if we interpret the (B,N,D) layout as (B,N,H,head_dim) with D=H*head_dim. The attention operation expects q as (B,H,N,head_dim). How is it stored? In the reference: `q = self.q_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)` So q becomes (B, H, N, head_dim) and contiguous (since transpose creates non-contiguous, but matmul may handle strides). Later, `logits = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1))` works with strides. In our fused kernel, we don't need to physically transpose if we can index correctly. If we store q as (B, N, D), then the element for head h, dim d at node i is at offset `i*D + h*head_dim + d`. This is exactly the same layout as (B, N, H, head_dim) with last dimension contiguous. Matmul in PyTorch with transpositions uses strides. But our custom kernel needs to know the layout. If we keep q as (B, N, D) and interpret it as: `q_val = q_ptr[b*N*D + i*D + h*head_dim + d]`. Is this equivalent to the transposed layout? Let's check the reference: Original after `.view(B, N, H, head_dim)`: memory is `(b, n, h, d)` contiguous. After `.transpose(1,2)`: shape (B, H, N, head_dim), strides: stride[0]=N*H*head_dim, stride[1]=head_dim, stride[2]=H*head_dim, stride[3]=1. So the element (b, h, n, d) is at offset `b*N*H*head_dim + h*head_dim + n*H*head_dim + d`. Wait, that's `b*N*D + n*D + h*head_dim + d`. Yes! Because stride for dimension 2 (n) is H*head_dim = D. So indeed, the transposed tensor is semantically (B,H,N,head_dim) but physically stored as (B,N,H,head_dim) with contiguous memory. This is because we transposed dimensions 1 and 2, but since the original view already had them as (N,H,head_dim), transposing to (H,N,head_dim) results in strides that are not monotonic? Let's compute carefully. Original `.view(B, N, H, head_dim)` has shape (B,N,H,head_dim) and strides (N*H*head_dim, H*head_dim, head_dim, 1). After `.transpose(1,2)`, shape becomes (B, H, N, head_dim), strides become (N*H*head_dim, head_dim, H*head_dim, 1). So the offset for index (b, h, n, d) is: `b * (N*H*head_dim) + h * head_dim + n * (H*head_dim) + d * 1` = `b*N*D + h*head_dim + n*D + d` = `b*N*D + n*D + h*head_dim + d`. So yes, the memory layout is the same as `(B,N,D)` with the head dimensions interleaved within the D dimension. Specifically, for node n, the D dimensions are grouped by head: [head0_d0..d_head_dim-1, head1_d0..., ...]. Therefore, storing q as (B,N,D) and interpreting it as described is correct. Great. So our Q, K, V buffers can just be (B,N,D). In the attention kernel, q_base for (b,h,i) should be: `q_base = b * N * D + i * D + h * head_dim;` And k_j for head h is: `k_base_j_d = b * N * D + j * D + h * head_dim + d;` Which simplifies to `k_ptr + b*N*D + j*D + h*head_dim + d`. So we can just pass q,k,v as (B,N,D) tensors. **Kernel 2 q/k/v indexing:** ```cpp int q_base = b * N * D + i * D + h * head_dim; ``` Then in loop over d: `q[q_base + d]` For k_j: `k[b * N * D + j * D + h * head_dim + d]` For v_j: `v[b * N * D + j * D + h * head_dim + d]` Wait, but `j` is `lane` for this thread. Each thread in the warp has a different j. So we compute: ```cpp int k_offset = b * N * D + lane * D + h * head_dim; v_offset similar. ``` Then in dot product loop: `sum += q[q_base + d] * k[k_offset + d];` This is good: all threads read the same q address (broadcast), and read k addresses spaced by D (strided). As discussed, it's okay. Actually, for v_offset, in the final accumulation: `v[v_offset + d]` where v_offset = b*N*D + lane*D + h*head_dim. Each thread reads its own v for its j. Good. **Kernel 1 output:** q, k, v are (B,N,D). We write directly. **Numerical equivalence:** The LayerNorm in PyTorch uses `eps=1e-5` by default. We should use the same. PyTorch softmax uses a numerically stable implementation. Our warp-level max and exp should match. PyTorch SiLU is `x * sigmoid(x)`. We implement as `tmp / (1.0f + expf(-tmp))` or `tmp * sigmoidf(tmp)`. `sigmoidf` might not be defined; we can use `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))`. Need to match fp32 exactly? Within tolerance. Differences due to order of operations in reduction might exist. For small sizes, it should be close. For LayerNorm, we compute variance as `mean((x - mean)^2)`. PyTorch uses the same. But there might be a slight difference in the reduction order. With 96 elements and warp shuffle, it should be very close. For the matmuls, our accumulation order may differ from cuBLAS. For small D=96, the difference should be within 1e-4. One potential issue: In the attention kernel, `max_logit` reduction: we do `float max_logit = dot;` and then shfl down. For lane >= N, dot = -1e30f. For lanes < N, dot is the actual value. The max across all 32 lanes will be the max across the 24 real lanes. Good. But `__shfl_down_sync` with `0xFFFFFFFF` includes all lanes. We need to make sure we don't use a narrower mask because inactive lanes (due to `if`) might not call the function. However, the call is outside the `if`. We call it unconditionally for all threads in the warp. Good. **Compiling and binding:** In Python, we will define the CUDA/C++ source string and call `load_inline` at module level. The functions list includes `fused_ln_qkv` and `fused_attn_out`. Let's write the code. **Kernel 1 code:** ```cuda __global__ void fused_ln_qkv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, const float* __restrict__ Wq, const float* __restrict__ Wk, const float* __restrict__ Wv, float* __restrict__ q, float* __restrict__ k, float* __restrict__ v, int B, int N, int D, float eps) { int b = blockIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_nodes = smem; float* s_w = smem + N * D; // Load nodes int total = N * D; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { s_nodes[idx] = nodes[b * total + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; for (int n_start = 0; n_start < N; n_start += num_warps) { int node = n_start + warp_id; if (node < N) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { sum += s_nodes[node * D + d]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } float mean = sum / D; float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { float diff = s_nodes[node * D + d] - mean; sq_sum += diff * diff; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq_sum, offset); } float var = sq_sum / D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { float val = (s_nodes[node * D + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * gamma[d] + beta[d]; s_nodes[node * D + d] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); // Projections auto proj = [&](const float* W, float* out_ptr) { // Load weight transposed into s_w with padding for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < D * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int d = idx / D; int k = idx % D; s_w[k * (D + 1) + d] = W[d * D + k]; } __syncthreads(); for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int kk = 0; kk < D; ++kk) { sum += s_nodes[n * D + kk] * s_w[kk * (D + 1) + d]; } out_ptr[b * total + idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); }; proj(Wq, q); proj(Wk, k); proj(Wv, v); } ``` Wait, a lambda inside a CUDA kernel is allowed in C++11 but not in `__global__` functions on all CUDA versions? In modern CUDA (>=11), lambda in device code is supported if compiled with `--expt-extended-lambda`. `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` passes appropriate flags? Usually it does. But to be safe, we can unroll the projection manually or write a device function. Or we can just write the code three times. It's a bit verbose but safe. Actually, a regular inline device function is safer. Let's write a device inline function or just repeat the code block. Repeating three times is fine. Actually, we can write a `__device__` inline function that takes `W` and `out_ptr`, but it needs access to `s_nodes`, `s_w`, `D`, `total`, `b`. We can pass them. ```cuda __device__ __forceinline__ void compute_proj( const float* s_nodes, float* s_w, int D, int total, int b, const float* W, float* out_ptr) { for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < D * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int d = idx / D; int k = idx % D; s_w[k * (D + 1) + d] = W[d * D + k]; } __syncthreads(); for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int kk = 0; kk < D; ++kk) { sum += s_nodes[n * D + kk] * s_w[kk * (D + 1) + d]; } out_ptr[b * total + idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Then call it three times in the kernel. But `__syncthreads()` inside a device function called by threads is okay as long as all threads in the block reach it, which they do because the loops are over all threads. However, `__syncthreads()` inside a non-`__global__` function can be tricky: all threads in the block must reach the same `__syncthreads()`. Since we call it inside the device function and all threads call the device function, it's fine. But it's generally discouraged? Actually, it's allowed if all threads in the block encounter it. Since we have `for (int idx = threadIdx.x; ...)` which all threads execute, it should be fine. But we need to ensure no early return. There is no early return. Good. However, putting `__syncthreads()` in an inline device function is fine if all threads in the block call it. Some style guides discourage it to avoid deadlocks, but it's legal. Let's keep it simple and repeat the code. **Kernel 2 code:** ```cuda __global__ void fused_attn_out_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ nodes_orig, const float* __restrict__ W1, const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ W2, const float* __restrict__ b2, const float* __restrict__ W_out, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int H, int P, float scale) { int b = blockIdx.x; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; int head_dim = D / H; int total_queries = H * N; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_ctx = smem; float* s_w = smem + N * D; // Initialize s_ctx to zero? We write all elements, so no need. // But we need to make sure all threads participate in writing. // Since total_queries*head_dim = H*N*head_dim = N*D = total elements. // Each query writes head_dim elements via lane 0. So all elements are written. for (int qid = warp_id; qid < total_queries; qid += num_warps) { int h = qid / N; int i = qid % N; int q_base = b * N * D + i * D + h * head_dim; int k_base = b * N * D + h * head_dim; // plus j*D int v_base = b * N * D + h * head_dim; // plus j*D // load pair feats for j = lane float pf[64]; int pf_base = ((b * N + i) * N + lane) * P; if (lane < N) { for (int p = 0; p < P; ++p) { pf[p] = pair_feats[pf_base + p]; } } float dot = -1e30f; if (lane < N) { int j = lane; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { sum += q[q_base + d] * k[k_base + j * D + d]; } float bias = b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; ++p) { float tmp = b1[p]; for (int q_idx = 0; q_idx < P; ++q_idx) { tmp += W1[p * P + q_idx] * pf[q_idx]; } // SiLU tmp = tmp / (1.0f + expf(-tmp)); bias += W2[h * P + p] * tmp; } dot = sum * scale + bias; float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N) + i) * N + j]; if (mask_val < 0.5f) dot = -1e30f; } float max_logit = dot; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_logit, offset); if (other > max_logit) max_logit = other; } float exp_val = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { exp_val = expf(dot - max_logit); } float sum_exp = exp_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_exp += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_exp, offset); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; float attn_weight = (lane < N) ? exp_val * inv_sum : 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float val = 0.0f; if (lane < N) { val = attn_weight * v[v_base + lane * D + d]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) { s_ctx[i * D + h * head_dim + d] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); // Load W_out transposed padded into s_w for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < D * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int d = idx / D; int k = idx % D; s_w[k * (D + 1) + d] = W_out[d * D + k]; } __syncthreads(); int total = N * D; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int kk = 0; kk < D; ++kk) { sum += s_ctx[n * D + kk] * s_w[kk * (D + 1) + d]; } float orig = nodes_orig[b * total + idx]; out[b * total + idx] = orig + sum; } } ``` Wait, in the out_proj, `s_w[k*(D+1) + d]` is transposed weight. `W_out[d*D + k]` is global weight. Yes. In `k_base + j * D + d` and `v_base + j * D + d`: since q,k,v are stored as (B,N,D) and head h's data is at offset `h*head_dim` within each node's D dims. So for node j, the base is `b*N*D + j*D + h*head_dim`. We wrote `k_base = b*N*D + h*head_dim` and then `j*D`. So `k_base + j*D + d` is correct. Wait, `q_base = b * N * D + i * D + h * head_dim`. Yes. `k_base = b * N * D + h * head_dim`. Then for j: `k_base + j * D + d`. This is `b*N*D + j*D + h*head_dim + d`. Correct. **C++ wrappers:** Need to wrap with PyTorch tensor API. ```cpp void fused_ln_qkv( at::Tensor nodes, at::Tensor gamma, at::Tensor beta, at::Tensor Wq, at::Tensor Wk, at::Tensor Wv, at::Tensor q, at::Tensor k, at::Tensor v) { int B = nodes.size(0); int N = nodes.size(1); int D = nodes.size(2); size_t smem_size = (N * D + D * (D + 1)) * sizeof(float); fused_ln_qkv_kernel<<<B, 256, smem_size>>>( nodes.data_ptr<float>(), gamma.data_ptr<float>(), beta.data_ptr<float>(), Wq.data_ptr<float>(), Wk.data_ptr<float>(), Wv.data_ptr<float>(), q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, D, 1e-5f); } void fused_attn_out( at::Tensor q, at::Tensor k, at::Tensor v, at::Tensor pair_feats, at::Tensor edge_mask, at::Tensor nodes_orig, at::Tensor W1, at::Tensor b1, at::Tensor W2, at::Tensor b2, at::Tensor W_out, at::Tensor out, float scale) { int B = q.size(0); int N = q.size(1); int D = q.size(2); int H = W2.size(0); // num_heads int P = W1.size(0); // pair_dim size_t smem_size = (N * D + D * (D + 1)) * sizeof(float); fused_attn_out_kernel<<<B, 256, smem_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), pair_feats.data_ptr<float>(), edge_mask.data_ptr<float>(), nodes_orig.data_ptr<float>(), W1.data_ptr<float>(), b1.data_ptr<float>(), W2.data_ptr<float>(), b2.data_ptr<float>(), W_out.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, D, H, P, scale); } ``` Wait, `W1.size(0)` is `pair_dim` (out_features of first linear). `W2.size(0)` is `num_heads`. This is a scene graph relation update operation. Residual add This is essentially a self-attention with pairwise edge bias. N=24 is quite small, so we have small matrices. B=4, H=4. Per edge: - Linear1: 16 multiplies, 15 adds = 31 ops - SiLU: a few ops - Linear2: 16 multiplies, 15 adds = 31 ops Total: ~70 ops per edge For one query, 24 edges: 1680 ops For all 24 queries: 40k ops Q*K^T per query: 24 dots of 24 dims = 24*24*2 = 1152 ops For all queries: 27k ops So rel_mlp is actually comparable in cost to Q*K^T! That's why fusing it is important. If we precompute the first layer, we still have the second layer: 16*2 = 32 ops per edge, total ~18k ops for all queries, which is significant but less. Out projection and residual Let me think about thread assignment within a block. Block size: 256 threads. For LayerNorm: 256 threads handling 24 nodes * 96 dims = 2304 elements. Each thread handles ~9 elements. We need block-level reductions for mean and variance. For QKV: 256 threads handling 24 nodes. We can assign each thread several (node, head_dim) elements. Actually, Q has shape (H, N, head_dim) = (4, 24, 24) = 2304 elements. With 256 threads, each thread computes 9 elements of Q. Each element is a dot product of node_norm (96 dims) with a column of W_q (96 dims). So each thread does 9 * 96 = 864 FMAs for Q, similarly for K and V. For rel_mlp: 576 edges. With 256 threads, some threads handle 2 edges, some handle 3. Each edge computation is small. For attention: for each head, 24 queries. Each query needs dot products with 24 keys + softmax + weighted sum with 24 values. Actually, if I structure the kernel properly, the block-level parallelism should work. Let me write a cleaner kernel structure: ```cuda __global__ void fused_gfvla_kernel( const float* nodes, // (B, N, D) const float* pair_feats, // (B, N, N, P) const float* edge_mask, // (B, N, N) const float* norm_w, // (D,) const float* norm_b, // (D,) const float* q_w, // (D, D) const float* k_w, // (D, D) const float* v_w, // (D, D) const float* rel_w1, // (P, P) const float* rel_b1, // (P,) const float* rel_w2, // (H, P) const float* rel_b2, // (H,) const float* out_w, // (D, D) float* output, // (B, N, D) int B, int N, int D, int P, int H ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // Shared memory layout // norm_nodes: N * D // q: H * N * head_dim // k: H * N * head_dim // v: H * N * head_dim // rel_bias: H * N * N // attn_scores: N * N (per head, reused) // softmax_sum: N (per head) // out_acc: N * D (accumulator before residual) extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* norm_nodes = smem; float* q = norm_nodes + N * D; float* k = q + H * N * head_dim; float* v = k + H * N * head_dim; float* rel_bias = v + H * N * head_dim; float* attn_scores = rel_bias + H * N * N; float* out_acc = attn_scores + N * N; // Step 1: LayerNorm for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; norm_nodes[idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute mean and variance per node - block-level reduction for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { // Each thread computes partial sum float sum = 0.0f; float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { float val = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; sum += val; sq_sum += val * val; } // Block reduction // ... use warp shuffle then shared memory float mean = ...; float var = ...; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { norm_nodes[n * D + d] = (norm_nodes[n * D + d] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: QKV projections // For each head, compute Q, K, V for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { for (int idx = tid; idx < N * head_dim; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; int col = h * head_dim + hd; float q_val = 0.0f, k_val = 0.0f, v_val = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float nd = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; q_val += nd * q_w[d * D + col]; k_val += nd * k_w[d * D + col]; v_val += nd * v_w[d * D + col]; } q[h * N * head_dim + idx] = q_val; k[h * N * head_dim + idx] = k_val; v[h * N * head_dim + idx] = v_val; } } __syncthreads(); // Step 3: rel_mlp for (int idx = tid; idx < H * N * N; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (N * N); int n = (idx / N) % N; int m = idx % N; // Read pair_feats[b, n, m, :] float interm[16]; // P=16 for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { interm[p] = pair_feats[b * N * N * P + n * N * P + m * P + p]; } // Linear1 float temp[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { temp[p] = rel_b1[p]; for (int pp = 0; pp < P; pp++) { temp[p] += interm[pp] * rel_w1[pp * P + p]; } } // SiLU for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { temp[p] = temp[p] * sigmoid(temp[p]); } // Linear2 float bias = rel_b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { bias += temp[p] * rel_w2[h * P + p]; } rel_bias[idx] = bias; } __syncthreads(); // Step 4: Attention per head for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { // Compute attention scores for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0.0f; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { score += q[h * N * head_dim + i * head_dim + hd] * k[h * N * head_dim + j * head_dim + hd]; } score *= scale; score += rel_bias[h * N * N + idx]; // Apply mask if (edge_mask[b * N * N + i * N + j] < 0.5f) { score = -1e9f; // or -INFINITY but careful with softmax } attn_scores[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { // Find max for numerical stability float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn_scores[i * N + j]); } // Reduce max across block // ... // Compute exp and sum float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { float e = expf(attn_scores[i * N + j] - max_val); attn_scores[i * N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } // Reduce sum // ... // Normalize for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { attn_scores[i * N + j] /= exp_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute context = attn @ v for (int idx = tid; idx < N * head_dim; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; float ctx = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { ctx += attn_scores[i * N + j] * v[h * N * head_dim + j * head_dim + hd]; } // Add to out_acc int out_idx = i * D + h * head_dim + hd; if (h == 0) out_acc[out_idx] = ctx; else out_acc[out_idx] += ctx; } __syncthreads(); } // Step 5: Output projection and residual for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float out_val = 0.0f; for (int dd = 0; dd < D; dd++) { out_val += out_acc[n * D + dd] * out_w[dd * D + d]; } output[b * N * D + idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx] + out_val; } } ``` Wait, this is getting very complicated with the block-level reductions. And I need to be very careful about shared memory layout and sizes. Let me compute shared memory: - norm_nodes: 2304 floats - q: 2304 floats - k: 2304 floats - v: 2304 floats - rel_bias: 2304 floats - attn_scores: 576 floats - out_acc: 2304 floats Total: 13440 floats = 53.8 KB That's well within limits. But the kernel is complex. Actually, some optimizations: 1. Don't need to store Q, K, V for all heads simultaneously if we process heads one by one. But we need Q and K for the attention matmul. Actually, for head-by-head, we'd recompute Q and K each time or store norm_nodes and recompute. Storing norm_nodes (2304 floats) is cheaper than Q+K+V (6912 floats). Wait, but we'd need to recompute Q, K, V for each head, which is redundant. Better to compute once and store, or compute and use immediately. Alternative approach: compute Q, K, V on the fly per head from norm_nodes. Since norm_nodes is in shared memory, and Q for one head is stored in registers or shared memory, this is fine. Let me redesign with head-by-head processing to reduce peak shared memory: ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* norm_nodes = smem; // N * D float* q = norm_nodes + N * D; // N * head_dim float* k = q + N * head_dim; // N * head_dim float* v = k + N * head_dim; // N * head_dim float* attn = v + N * head_dim; // N * N float* out_acc = attn + N * N; // N * D // Load and normalize nodes ... // Initialize out_acc to 0 ... for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { // Compute Q, K, V for this head for (int idx = tid; idx < N * head_dim; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; int col = h * head_dim + hd; float qv = 0, kv = 0, vv = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float nd = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; qv += nd * q_w[d * D + col]; kv += nd * k_w[d * D + col]; vv += nd * v_w[d * D + col]; } q[idx] = qv; k[idx] = kv; v[idx] = vv; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores + rel_bias for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { score += q[i * head_dim + hd] * k[j * head_dim + hd]; } score *= scale; // Compute rel_bias on the fly // Read pair_feats, do rel_mlp float interm[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { interm[p] = pair_feats[b*N*N*P + i*N*P + j*P + p]; } // linear1 + silu float tmp[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { tmp[p] = rel_b1[p]; for (int pp = 0; pp < P; pp++) { tmp[p] += interm[pp] * rel_w1[pp*P + p]; } float s = tmp[p]; tmp[p] = s / (1.0f + expf(-s)); // SiLU = x * sigmoid(x) -- wait // Actually SiLU(x) = x * sigmoid(x) tmp[p] = s * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-s))); } // linear2 float bias = rel_b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { bias += tmp[p] * rel_w2[h*P + p]; } score += bias; if (edge_mask[b*N*N + i*N + j] < 0.5f) { score = -INFINITY; // careful with exp later } attn[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { // Block reduction for max float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i*N + j]); } // warp reduce... // shared reduce... float row_max = ...; float exp_sum = 0; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { float e = expf(attn[i*N + j] - row_max); attn[i*N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } // reduce sum... float sum = ...; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { attn[i*N + j] /= sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Context for (int idx = tid; idx < N * head_dim; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; float ctx = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { ctx += attn[i*N + j] * v[j * head_dim + hd]; } // Atomic add to out_acc or accumulate atomicAdd(&out_acc[i * D + h * head_dim + hd], ctx); } __syncthreads(); } // Output projection and residual ... } ``` This gets really complex with the block reductions for softmax, and shared memory layout needs to be precise. Let me see if I can make it simpler by using a different thread organization. Actually, for N=24 and head_dim=24, another approach is to have each warp process one query row. So kernel 2 can be: ```cuda for each (b, h) block: load q, k, v from global memory compute attention with on-the-fly rel_bias compute context for each (n, d): partial = sum_hd context[n, hd] * W_out[h*head_dim + hd, d] atomicAdd(&out_buffer[b, n, d], partial) ``` Then after all kernel 2 blocks finish, a tiny kernel or even PyTorch op does `nodes + out_buffer`. (B, H, N, D) = 4*4*24*96*4 = 147 KB. Very small. And the reduction kernel has N*D=2304 work items per batch, with B=4 batches. Could be a simple kernel. So the final design: Kernel 1: `fused_norm_qkv` - B blocks, each with T threads - Loads nodes for batch b - Computes LayerNorm - Computes Q, K, V projections - Writes to q, k, v buffers Kernel 2: `fused_attention_per_head` - B * H blocks - For each (b, h): - Load q, k, v - Compute rel_bias on the fly from pair_feats - Compute attention, softmax - Compute context = attn @ v - Compute out_partial = context @ W_out_slice - Write out_partial[b, h, n, d] for all n, d Kernel 3: `sum_heads_and_add_residual` - B blocks, each handling N * D elements - Sum over heads: out[b, n, d] = nodes[b, n, d] + sum_h out_partial[b, h, n, d] This is clean and parallelizable. Per batch, total attention work: - Q*K^T: 4 * 24 * 24 * 24 = 55,296 FMAs - Softmax: minimal - Attn*V: 4 * 24 * 24 * 24 = 55,296 FMAs - rel_mlp: 576 * 4 * (16*16*2 + 16*4*2) ≈ 576*4*576 = 1.3M? At 67 TFLOPS, this is ~8 microseconds. But memory bandwidth and kernel launch overhead will dominate. With 4 blocks, each doing 516k FMAs, total is 2M FMAs. For LayerNorm mean and variance: ```cuda for each node n: float sum = 0, sq_sum = 0; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float val = nodes[b*N*D + n*D + d]; smem[n*D + d] = val; sum += val; sq_sum += val * val; } // block reduction for (int offset = blockDim.x/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq_sum, offset); } // broadcast from lane 0 if (tid % 32 == 0) { s_sum[tid/32] = sum; s_sq_sum[tid/32] = sq_sum; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { sum = (tid < (blockDim.x+31)/32) ? s_sum[tid] : 0; sq_sum = (tid < (blockDim.x+31)/32) ? s_sq_sum[tid] : 0; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq_sum, offset); } } __syncthreads(); // Now lane 0 in warp 0 has the full sum and sq_sum if (tid == 0) { s_mean[n] = sum / D; s_var[n] = sq_sum / D - s_mean[n] * s_mean[n]; } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean[n]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var[n] + 1e-5f); for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float val = smem[n*D + d]; smem[n*D + d] = (val - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } ``` Wait, this has a bug - I need to be careful with warp-level vs block-level reductions. Actually, I can simplify using cooperative groups or just use __syncthreads-based reduction in shared memory. For simplicity and correctness, let me just use shared memory for reductions: ```cuda // Block-level sum reduction __device__ float block_sum(float val, float* shared) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); if (lane_id == 0) shared[warp_id] = val; __syncthreads(); // Reduce across warps if (tid < 32) { val = (tid < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? shared[tid] : 0; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } ``` Hmm, but only lane 0 of each warp in the second reduction participates. It's tricky. Let me use a simpler shared memory approach: ```cuda // Even simpler: all threads write to shared, then tree reduction // But with 256 threads, this takes 8 steps shared[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) shared[tid] += shared[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } float total = shared[0]; ``` This is clean and works for any block size. It's slightly slower than warp shuffle but for D=96 and N=24, who cares? Similarly for softmax, we need max and sum per row of 24 elements. With 256 threads and block-level syncs, we can do: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { // Find max float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i*N + j]); shared[tid] = max_val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) shared[tid] = fmaxf(shared[tid], shared[tid + s]); __syncthreads(); } float row_max = shared[0]; // Compute exp and sum float exp_sum = 0; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { float e = expf(attn[i*N + j] - row_max); attn[i*N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } shared[tid] = exp_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) shared[tid] += shared[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } float row_sum = shared[0]; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) attn[i*N + j] /= row_sum; } ``` This is clean and correct. Now let me think about the QKV projection. With norm_nodes in shared memory and weight matrices in global memory, each thread computes some elements of Q, K, V. Actually, I realize I should also think about weight matrix access patterns. The weight matrices are: - q_w: (D, D) = (96, 96) = 9216 floats When computing Q[b, h, n, hd] = sum_d norm_nodes[b, n, d] * q_w[d, h*head_dim + hd] To maximize cache efficiency, threads that compute elements with the same d should load the corresponding norm_nodes value together. Actually, with D=96 and head_dim=24, and H=4: Thread 0 computes Q[b,0,0,0] = dot(norm_nodes[0,:], q_w[:,0]) Thread 1 computes Q[b,0,0,1] = dot(norm_nodes[0,:], q_w[:,1]) ... Thread 23 computes Q[b,0,0,23] = dot(norm_nodes[0,:], q_w[:,23]) Thread 24 computes Q[b,0,1,0] = dot(norm_nodes[1,:], q_w[:,0]) etc. Doing these as nested loops is fine for small sizes. The weights will be cached in L1 since threads access nearby columns. Similarly for rel_mlp: pair_feats is (B, N, N, P), accessed as pair_feats[b, i, j, :]. For edge (i,j), all threads computing different heads need the same pair_feats values. Might as well compute it on the fly as needed. With 256 threads and 576 edges, edges are distributed across threads, so each thread computes a few edges. That's fine. OK let me write the kernel more carefully. Let me trace memory: Kernel 1 reads nodes (36 KB) and writes q, k, v (110 KB total) Kernel 2 reads q, k, v (110 KB), pair_feats (147 KB), edge_mask (9 KB), and writes context_per_head (147 KB) Kernel 3 reads context_per_head (147 KB) and nodes (36 KB), writes output (36 KB) Total global memory: ~600 KB. Still tiny. And kernel 2 has 16 blocks, which is much better for SM utilization. Let me design this 3-kernel approach: Kernel 1: `fused_norm_qkv` ```cuda __global__ void fused_norm_qkv( const float* nodes, const float* norm_w, const float* norm_b, const float* q_w, const float* k_w, const float* v_w, float* q_out, float* k_out, float* v_out, int B, int N, int D, int H, int head_dim ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* norm_nodes = smem; // Load nodes for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += blockDim.x) { norm_nodes[idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { float sum = 0, sq_sum = 0; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float val = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; sum += val; sq_sum += val * val; } // block reduction sum = blockReduceSum(sum); sq_sum = blockReduceSum(sq_sum); float mean = sum / D; float var = sq_sum / D - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float val = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; norm_nodes[n * D + d] = (val - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } __syncthreads(); } // QKV projections int total_qkv = H * N * head_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_qkv; idx += blockDim.x) { int h = idx / (N * head_dim); int n = (idx / head_dim) % N; int hd = idx % head_dim; int w_col = h * head_dim + hd; float qv = 0, kv = 0, vv = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float nd = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; qv += nd * q_w[d * D + w_col]; kv += nd * k_w[d * D + w_col]; vv += nd * v_w[d * D + w_col]; } q_out[b * H * N * head_dim + idx] = qv; k_out[b * H * N * head_dim + idx] = kv; v_out[b * H * N * head_dim + idx] = vv; } } ``` Kernel 2: `fused_attention_per_head` ```cuda __global__ void fused_attention_per_head( const float* q_in, const float* k_in, const float* v_in, const float* pair_feats, const float* edge_mask, const float* rel_w1, const float* rel_b1, const float* rel_w2, const float* rel_b2, float* context_out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, int head_dim, float scale ) { int b = blockIdx.x / H; int h = blockIdx.x % H; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* q = smem; float* k = q + N * head_dim; float* v = k + N * head_dim; float* attn = v + N * head_dim; // Load Q, K, V for (int idx = tid; idx < N * head_dim; idx += blockDim.x) { q[idx] = q_in[b * H * N * head_dim + h * N * head_dim + idx]; k[idx] = k_in[b * H * N * head_dim + h * N * head_dim + idx]; v[idx] = v_in[b * H * N * head_dim + h * N * head_dim + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores + rel_bias for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { score += q[i * head_dim + hd] * k[j * head_dim + hd]; } score *= scale; // rel_mlp float interm[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { interm[p] = pair_feats[b * N * N * P + i * N * P + j * P + p]; } float tmp[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { tmp[p] = rel_b1[p]; for (int pp = 0; pp < P; pp++) { tmp[p] += interm[pp] * rel_w1[pp * P + p]; } // SiLU tmp[p] = tmp[p] / (1.0f + expf(-tmp[p])); // sigmoid tmp[p] = tmp[p] * (tmp[p] / (1.0f + expf(-tmp[p]))); // wait no // SiLU(x) = x * sigmoid(x) float s = tmp[p]; tmp[p] = s * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-s))); } float bias = rel_b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { bias += tmp[p] * rel_w2[h * P + p]; } score += bias; if (edge_mask[b * N * N + i * N + j] < 0.5f) { score = -1e9f; } attn[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i * N + j]); } max_val = blockReduceMax(max_val); float exp_sum = 0; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { float e = expf(attn[i * N + j] - max_val); attn[i * N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } exp_sum = blockReduceSum(exp_sum); for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { attn[i * N + j] /= exp_sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Context = attn @ v for (int idx = tid; idx < N * head_dim; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; float ctx = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { ctx += attn[i * N + j] * v[j * head_dim + hd]; } context_out[b * H * N * head_dim + h * N * head_dim + idx] = ctx; } } ``` Wait, I can simplify. Instead of writing context_out and then doing output projection separately, I can do output projection directly in kernel 2! Each block computes context for one head, which is (N, head_dim). Then it computes out_partial = context @ W_out[head_start:head_end, :] and atomically adds to a global accumulator. Kernel 2 modified: ```cuda __global__ void fused_attention_out( ... const float* out_w, float* out_acc, // (B, N, D), initialized to 0 ... ) { ... // After computing context for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float partial = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { partial += context[n * head_dim + hd] * out_w[(h * head_dim + hd) * D + d]; } atomicAdd(&out_acc[b * N * D + idx], partial); } } ``` And then kernel 3 just does `output = nodes + out_acc`. This saves writing/reading the context buffer! Total global memory with this approach: Kernel 1: read nodes (36KB), write qkv (110KB) Kernel 2: read qkv (110KB), pair_feats (147KB), edge_mask (9KB), write out_acc (36KB, with atomic adds so read-modify-write) Kernel 3: read out_acc (36KB), nodes (36KB), write output (36KB) Total: ~556 KB. Very small. Wait, atomic adds on global memory are more expensive than simple writes. But with 4 atomic adds per element and good cache behavior, should be fine. Read it once in reduction: 147 KB. Total traffic: ~700 KB. Similar. And atomics might be okay for 16 blocks. Hmm, but atomics on the same memory locations from 4 blocks can have contention. The L2 cache should handle it since each batch's out_acc is accessed by only 4 blocks. And within a batch, the 4 blocks write to different (n, d) elements. So minimal contention. Actually wait, the 4 blocks for the same batch all write to the same out_acc slice. If they happen to write the same (n, d) element at the same time, there is contention. Let me check: For one block per batch with head-by-head processing: - norm_nodes: 2304 floats = 9.2 KB - q: 576 floats = 2.3 KB - k: 576 floats = 2.3 KB - v: 576 floats = 2.3 KB - attn: 576 floats = 2.3 KB - out_acc: 2304 floats = 9.2 KB - plus temporary arrays for reductions Total: ~30 KB 30 KB * 4 blocks = 120 KB shared memory used simultaneously. H100 has 228 KB per SM, so 1 block per SM fits easily. We could even have 2 blocks per SM if we wanted. With 4 blocks spread across 4 SMs (out of 132), each SM runs one block. The other SMs are idle. But that's fine - we only have 4 units of work. Wait, CUDA runtime might not spread them to 4 different SMs. It depends on scheduling. But either way, having 4 blocks isn't great for latency hiding because if one block stalls on a memory operation, there's no other block on the same SM to switch to. With 16 blocks, we have more opportunities for latency hiding. The scheduler can run a different block while one waits for memory. So maybe having 16 blocks is better even with more memory traffic? For 16 blocks spread across, say, 16 SMs, we still have many idle SMs. But within an SM, if multiple blocks can run concurrently, we get better latency hiding. Actually, on H100, each SM can run multiple blocks if resources allow. With shared memory of 30 KB per block and 228 KB available, an SM can run up to 7 blocks. With 16 blocks total, they can pack into 3 SMs (each running ~5 blocks) or spread across 16 SMs (1 block each). I think having 16 blocks is generally better for latency hiding. Let me compromise: I'll precompute LayerNorm in one small kernel (or even use PyTorch for it, since it's just one kernel and efficient). Wait, for the SiLU gradient, I need to make sure I implement it correctly: SiLU(x) = x * sigmoid(x) = x / (1 + exp(-x)) For negative x with large magnitude, exp(-x) can overflow. So: sigmoid(x) = 1 / (1 + exp(-x)) For x < 0, exp(-x) = exp(|x|) which can overflow. Better: sigmoid(x) = exp(x) / (1 + exp(x)) for x < 0. Or just use the fast approximation: ```cpp inline float sigmoid(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } ``` For x = -88, exp(88) overflows in float. For x = 88, exp(-88) underflows to 0. So sigmoid(88) = 1, sigmoid(-88) = 0. The intermediate values might overflow. To be safe: ```cpp inline float sigmoid(float x) { if (x >= 0) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } else { float e = expf(x); return e / (1.0f + e); } } ``` For SiLU: `x * sigmoid(x)`. When x = -88, sigmoid(x) = 0, so SiLU(x) = 0, no overflow. Let me use this stable sigmoid. Now, for the kernel code, I'll also need block reduction functions. Let me write them carefully. Actually, to keep things simple and correct, for reductions with small N=24, I might not need warp shuffles. A simple loop-based reduction in shared memory with __syncthreads is fine. ```cpp __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val, float* shared) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int blockSize = blockDim.x; shared[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockSize / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { shared[tid] += shared[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } return shared[0]; } ``` Wait, this doesn't handle block sizes that aren't powers of 2. But I'll use blockDim.x = 256 which is a power of 2. For the reduction to be correct, I need all threads in the block to participate in __syncthreads. Actually, `blockReduceSum` with a tree reduction in shared memory is fine as long as the block size is a power of 2. If blockDim.x = 256: - s = 128: threads 0..127 add shared[128..255] to shared[0..127] - s = 64: threads 0..63 add shared[64..127] to shared[0..63] - ... until s = 1: thread 0 adds shared[1] to shared[0] Then all threads can read shared[0]. // N * head_dim float* v = k + N * head_dim; // N * head_dim float* attn = v + N * head_dim; // N * N float* scratch = attn + N * N; // blockDim.x // Load normalized nodes for this batch for (int idx = tid; I made an indexing error. For `nn.Linear(in, out)` with weight shape (out, in), the element is `W[out_idx, in_idx]` at `W[out_idx * in + in_idx]`. Wait, but PyTorch uses row-major: `tensor[i, j]` is at `tensor[i * stride0 + j * stride1]`. For a contiguous 2D tensor, `stride1 = 1`, so `tensor[i, j]` is at `tensor[i * dim1 + j]`. OK, continuing: ```cpp // Compute attention scores with rel_bias for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int hd = 0; } attn[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { // Find max float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i * N + j]); exp_sum += e; } exp_sum = block_reduce_sum(exp_sum, scratch); // Normalize for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { attn[i * N + j] /= exp_sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Compute context = attn @ v, store in q (reuse) for (int idx = tid; idx < qkv_size; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; float ctx = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { ctx += attn[n * N + j] * v[j * head_dim + hd]; } q[idx] = ctx; // Reuse q as context buffer } __syncthreads(); // Output projection and atomic add for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float partial = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { partial += q[n * head_dim + hd] * out_w[d * D + h * head_dim + hd]; } atomicAdd(&out_acc[b * N * D + idx], partial); } } ``` Wait, there's a potential issue with the block_reduce functions. The `block_reduce_max` and `block_reduce_sum` functions use `__syncthreads()`. When called inside the softmax loop over N rows, for each row we call block_reduce_max and then block_reduce_sum. These functions internally call `__syncthreads()`, and the loop itself has a `__syncthreads()` at the end. In CUDA, all threads in a block must execute `__syncthreads()`. The functions do this correctly. But the functions also have `return shared[0]` at the end, and `__syncthreads()` is called before reading `shared[0]`. Actually, `return shared[0]` after the final `__syncthreads()` is fine because all threads have synchronized and written to shared[0]. Wait, but in `block_reduce_sum`, after the final `__syncthreads()`, all threads return `shared[0]`. That's correct because shared[0] has the final result. However, there's a subtle issue: in the loop `for (int i = 0; i < N; i++)`, all threads must participate in the loop. If some threads skip the loop body because of a condition near the beginning of the loop, they might not reach the `__syncthreads()` at the end. In my code, `for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads)` - all threads participate. And both block_reduce_* functions have __syncthreads(). So all threads should reach all sync points. Reduction kernel can be simple: ```cpp __global__ void reduce_and_add_residual( const float* partials, // (B, H, N, D) const float* nodes, float* output, int B, int N, int D, int H ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * N * D; if (idx >= total) return; float sum = 0.0f; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { sum += partials[idx * H + h]; // Wait, need correct indexing } output[idx] = nodes[idx] + sum; } ``` Actually, the layout matters. Let me draft the complete Python file: ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_CODE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> inline __device__ float sigmoid_fast(float x) { if (x >= 0.0f) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } else { float e = expf(x); return e / (1.0f + e); } } __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val, float* shared) { int tid = threadIdx.x; shared[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x >> 1; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { shared[tid] += shared[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } return shared[0]; } __device__ float block_reduce_max(float val, float* shared) { int tid = threadIdx.x; shared[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x >> 1; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { shared[tid] = fmaxf(shared[tid], shared[tid + s]); } __syncthreads(); } return shared[0]; } __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ q_w, const float* __restrict__ k_w, const float* __restrict__ v_w, const float* __restrict__ rel_w1, const float* __restrict__ rel_b1, const float* __restrict__ rel_w2, const float* __restrict__ rel_b2, const float* __restrict__ out_w, float* __restrict__ partials, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, int head_dim, float scale ) { int b = blockIdx.x / H; int h = blockIdx.x % H; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* norm_nodes = smem; float* q = norm_nodes + N * D; float* k = q + N * head_dim; float* v = k + N * head_dim; float* attn = v + N * head_dim; float* scratch = attn + N * N; // Load normalized nodes for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { norm_nodes[idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q, K, V for this head int qkv_size = N * head_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < qkv_size; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; int w_col = h * head_dim + hd; float qv = 0.0f, kv = 0.0f, vv = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float nd = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; qv += nd * q_w[w_col * D + d]; kv += nd * k_w[w_col * D + d]; vv += nd * v_w[w_col * D + d]; } q[idx] = qv; k[idx] = kv; v[idx] = vv; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores + rel_bias for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0.0f; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { score += q[i * head_dim + hd] * k[j * head_dim + hd]; } score *= scale; // rel_mlp on pair_feats[b, i, j, :] float pf[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { pf[p] = pair_feats[b * N * N * P + i * N * P + j * P + p]; } float tmp[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { float val = rel_b1[p]; for (int pp = 0; pp < P; pp++) { val += pf[pp] * rel_w1[p * P + pp]; } val = val * sigmoid_fast(val); tmp[p] = val; } float bias = rel_b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { bias += tmp[p] * rel_w2[h * P + p]; } score += bias; if (edge_mask[b * N * N + i * N + j] < 0.5f) { score = -1e9f; } attn[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i * N + j]); } max_val = block_reduce_max(max_val, scratch); float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { float e = expf(attn[i * N + j] - max_val); attn[i * N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } exp_sum = block_reduce_sum(exp_sum, scratch); for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { attn[i * N + j] /= exp_sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Context = attn @ v, reuse q as context buffer for (int idx = tid; idx < qkv_size; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; float ctx = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { ctx += attn[n * N + j] * v[j * head_dim + hd]; } q[idx] = ctx; } __syncthreads(); // Output projection partial for this head for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float partial = 0.0f; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { partial += q[n * head_dim + hd] * out_w[d * D + h * head_dim + hd]; } int partial_idx = ((b * H + h) * N + n) * D + d; partials[partial_idx] = partial; } } __global__ void reduce_and_residual_kernel( const float* __restrict__ partials, const float* __restrict__ nodes, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int D, int H ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * N * D; if (idx >= total) return; int b = idx / (N * D); int rem = idx % (N * D); int n = rem / D; int d = rem % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { sum += partials[((b * H + h) * N + n) * D + d]; } output[idx] = nodes[idx] + sum; } ''' class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, num_heads: int): super().__init__() assert dim % num_heads == 0 self.dim = dim self.pair_dim = pair_dim self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = dim // num_heads self.scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(self.head_dim) self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim) self.q_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False) self.k_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False) self.rel_mlp = nn.Sequential( nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim), nn.SiLU(), nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads), ) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False) # Compile CUDA extension self._cuda_ext = load_inline( name="gfvla_fused", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=CUDA_CODE, functions=["fused_attention_kernel", "reduce_and_residual_kernel"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) def forward(self, nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask): B, N, D = nodes.shape P = self.pair_dim H = self.num_heads head_dim = self.head_dim h = self.norm(nodes) # Allocate partial output buffer partials = torch.zeros(B, H, N, D, device=nodes.device, dtype=nodes.dtype) BLOCK_SIZE = 256 grid1 = (B * H,) block1 = (BLOCK_SIZE,) shared_mem = (N * D + 3 * N * head_dim + N * N + BLOCK_SIZE) * 4 self._cuda_ext.fused_attention_kernel( h, pair_feats, edge_mask, self.q_proj.weight, self.k_proj.weight, self.v_proj.weight, self.rel_mlp[0].weight, self.rel_mlp[0].bias, self.rel_mlp[2].weight, self.rel_mlp[2].bias, self.out_proj.weight, partials, B, N, D, P, H, head_dim, self.scale, grid=grid1, block=block1, shared_mem=shared_mem ) output = torch.empty_like(nodes) total_elems = B * N * D grid2 = ((total_elems + BLOCK_SIZE - 1) // BLOCK_SIZE,) block2 = (BLOCK_SIZE,) self._cuda_ext.reduce_and_residual_kernel( partials, nodes, output, B, N, D, H, grid=grid2, block=block2 ) return output ``` Wait, I need to check the weight tensor shapes and ensure they're contiguous.
