Actually, I just realized there's an even simpler 2-kernel approach: - Kernel 1: Fused QKV + attention for all heads, one block per batch. Output is (B, N, D). - Kernel 2: Output projection + residual, one block per batch. For kernel 1: 1. Load normalized nodes 2. Compute Q, K, V for all heads 3. For each head: a. Compute attention (reuse attn SMEM) b. Compute context and accumulate into out_acc (N, D) 4. Write out_acc For kernel 2: 1. Load out_acc 2. out = nodes + out_acc @ out_w^T Wait, but then kernel 2 is just another matmul + add, which is what PyTorch does efficiently. Actually, I can include the output projection in kernel 1. For kernel 1 with output projection included: 1. Load normalized nodes 2. Compute Q, K, V for all heads 3. Initialize out_acc to 0 in shared memory 4. For each head: a. Compute attention b. Compute context c. partial_out = context @ out_w[head_start:head_end, :] d. Add partial_out to out_acc 5. Write out_acc to global memory Then kernel 2: `output = nodes + out_acc` This is still one fused kernel per batch (4 blocks) plus a simple add kernel. The main issue with this design is that for step 4c, `partial_out` is (N, D), computed from context (N, head_dim) and out_w_slice (head_dim, D). This is a matmul of size (24, 24) @ (24, 96). For each head. I can compute this efficiently with the 256 threads. OK let me write this version. It's cleaner and avoids partials buffer. ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, // normalized nodes (B, N, D) const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ q_w, const float* __restrict__ k_w, const float* __restrict__ v_w, const float* __restrict__ rel_w1, const float* __restrict__ rel_b1, const float* __restrict__ rel_w2, const float* __restrict__ rel_b2, const float* __restrict__ out_w, float* __restrict__ out_acc, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, int head_dim, float scale ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* norm_nodes = smem; float* qkv = norm_nodes + N * D; // 3 * H * N * head_dim float* attn = qkv + 3 * H * N * head_dim; // N * N float* out_smem = attn + N * N; // N * D float* scratch = out_smem + N * D; // blockDim.x // Load normalized nodes for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { norm_nodes[idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q, K, V for all heads int total_qkv = H * N * head_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_qkv; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (N * head_dim); int n = (idx / head_dim) % N; int hd = idx % head_dim; int w_col = h * head_dim + hd; float* q_ptr = qkv + 0 * total_qkv; float* k_ptr = qkv + 1 * total_qkv; float* v_ptr = qkv + 2 * total_qkv; float qv = 0, kv = 0, vv = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float nd = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; qv += nd * q_w[w_col * D + d]; kv += nd * k_w[w_col * D + d]; vv += nd * v_w[w_col * D + d]; } q_ptr[idx] = qv; k_ptr[idx] = kv; v_ptr[idx] = vv; } __syncthreads(); // Initialize out_smem to 0 for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { out_smem[idx] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Attention for each head float* q_ptr = qkv + 0 * total_qkv; float* k_ptr = qkv + 1 * total_qkv; float* v_ptr = qkv + 2 * total_qkv; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { // Compute attention scores for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { int q_idx = h * N * head_dim + i * head_dim + hd; int k_idx = h * N * head_dim + j * head_dim + hd; score += q_ptr[q_idx] * k_ptr[k_idx]; } score *= scale; // rel_mlp float pf[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { pf[p] = pair_feats[b * N * N * P + i * N * P + j * P + p]; } float tmp[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { float val = rel_b1[p]; for (int pp = 0; pp < P; pp++) { val += pf[pp] * rel_w1[p * P + pp]; } val = val * sigmoid_fast(val); tmp[p] = val; } float bias = rel_b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { bias += tmp[p] * rel_w2[h * P + p]; } score += bias; if (edge_mask[b * N * N + i * N + j] < 0.5f) { score = -1e9f; } attn[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i * N + j]); } max_val = block_reduce_max(max_val, scratch); float exp_sum = 0; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { float e = expf(attn[i * N + j] - max_val); attn[i * N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } exp_sum = block_reduce_sum(exp_sum, scratch); for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { attn[i * N + j] /= exp_sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Context = attn @ v, then add to out_smem via output projection for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float ctx = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { float attn_val = attn[n * N + j]; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { ctx += attn_val * v_ptr[h * N * head_dim + j * head_dim + hd] * out_w[d * D + h * head_dim + hd]; } } out_smem[idx] += ctx; } __syncthreads(); } // Write out_smem to global memory for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { out_acc[b * N * D + idx] = out_smem[idx]; } } ``` Wait! The context + output projection can be combined: `partial_out[n, d] = sum_j attn[n, j] * sum_hd v[j, hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim + hd]` `= sum_j attn[n, j] * v_proj[j, d]` Where `v_proj[j, d] = sum_hd v[j, hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim + hd]` I can precompute `v_proj` for all j, d, then do `out_smem += attn @ v_proj`. For each head, `v_proj` is (N, D) = (24, 96). Computing it: ```cpp for (int jd = 0; jd < N * D; jd++) { int j = jd / D; int d = jd % D; float vp = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { vp += v_ptr[...] * out_w[...]; } v_proj[jd] = vp; } ``` Then: ```cpp for (int nd = 0; nd < N * D; nd++) { int n = nd / D; int d = nd % D; float out_val = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { out_val += attn[n * N + j] * v_proj[j * D + d]; } out_smem[nd] += out_val; } ``` This is two separate loops. The first computes v_proj (N*D*head_dim FMAs = 55k), the second computes the sum (N*N*D FMAs = 55k). Total 110k FMAs per head. My earlier combined approach: ```cpp for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float ctx = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { float attn_val = attn[n * N + j]; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { ctx += attn_val * v_ptr[...] * out_w[...]; } } out_smem[idx] += ctx; } ``` This computes the same thing but in one triple loop. The number of FMAs is the same (N * N * D * head_dim = 24*24*96*24 = 1.3M per head). Wait, that's 1.3M FMAs, not 110k! My decomposition was wrong. Let me recalculate: `partial_out[n, d] = sum_j attn[n,j] * sum_hd v[j,hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim+hd]` `= sum_j sum_hd attn[n,j] * v[j,hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim+hd]` This is indeed N * N * head_dim * D operations if done naively. With N=24, head_dim=24, D=96: 24 * 24 * 24 * 96 = 1,327,104 FMAs per head! For H=4 heads: 5.3M FMAs. But wait, if I factorize: `v_proj[j, d] = sum_hd v[j,hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim+hd]` - this is N*D*head_dim = 24*96*24 = 55,296 FMAs Then `out[n, d] = sum_j attn[n,j] * v_proj[j, d]` - this is N*N*D = 24*24*96 = 55,296 FMAs Total: 110,592 FMAs per head! My earlier combined code was wrong - it had a triple nested loop that did redundant work. I should use the factorized approach. In the factorized approach, I need to store v_proj somewhere. v_proj for one head is N*D = 2304 floats = 9.2 KB. I can store it in shared memory. Shared memory layout: - norm_nodes: 2304 - qkv: 3*H*N*head_dim = 6912 - attn: 576 - v_proj: 2304 - out_smem: 2304 - scratch: 256 Total: 14656 floats = 58.6 KB Still fine. But wait, I can reuse qkv memory for v_proj, since v for the current head is still needed but q and k for the current head can be overwritten... Actually, no. v_proj needs v for the current head. But q and k for the current head are no longer needed after the attention scores are computed. So I can use q's space or k's space for v_proj. q for one head is N*head_dim = 576 floats. v_proj is 2304 floats. So q's space is too small. Actually, I can reuse the entire qkv space! After computing attention, q and k are not needed. But v is needed for v_proj. Once v_proj is computed, v is also not needed directly. Let me think: 1. After softmax, q and k are not needed. 2. We need v to compute v_proj. v_proj = v @ W_out_slice. 3. Once v_proj is computed, v is not needed. 4. Then out += attn @ v_proj. I can compute v_proj and store it in the qkv space, overwriting q and k. qkv space is 3*H*N*head_dim = 6912 floats. v_proj for one head is N*D = 2304 floats. So I can store v_proj in, say, the first 2304 floats of qkv. After computing v_proj for head h, I overwrite q space. Then compute out += attn @ v_proj. For the next head, I need q, k, v again. But I've overwritten q and k! So I can't reuse qkv across heads unless I compute q, k, v for all heads upfront and then process heads sequentially. Actually, I can compute Q, K, V for all heads once, store them in qkv. Then for each head h, I use q[h], k[h], v[h] to compute attention, then compute v_proj from v[h], store v_proj somewhere, and accumulate out_smem. If I store v_proj in the q[0] and k[0] slots for the current head, that would only give me 2*N*head_dim = 1152 floats. But v_proj needs N*D = 2304 floats. Alternatively, I can compute v_proj and accumulate out_smem on the fly without storing full v_proj: For each n, d: `out[n,d] += sum_j attn[n,j] * sum_hd v[j,hd] * out_w[d, ...]` We can compute this as: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { float a = attn[n * N + j]; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { float v_val = v_ptr[h * N * head_dim + j * head_dim + hd]; float w_val = out_w[d * D + h * head_dim + hd]; out_smem[n * D + d] += a * v_val * w_val; } } ``` But this is N*N*D*head_dim FMAs, which is 1.3M per head. That's inefficient. Wait, I miscalculated. Let me re-examine. The efficient factorization: `partial_out[n,d] = sum_j attn[n,j] * (sum_hd v[j,hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim+hd])` `= sum_j attn[n,j] * v_proj[j,d]` Where v_proj[j,d] = sum_hd v[j,hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim+hd] Computing v_proj: for each (j,d), dot product of head_dim elements. - FMAs: N * D * head_dim = 24 * 96 * 24 = 55,296 Computing partial_out: for each (n,d), dot product of N elements. - FMAs: N * D * N = 24 * 96 * 24 = 55,296 Total: 110,592 FMAs per head. For 4 heads: 442k FMAs. The naive approach: For each (n,d), triple sum over j and hd: - FMAs: N * D * N * head_dim = 24 * 96 * 24 * 24 = 1,327,104 per head. For 4 heads: 5.3M FMAs. So the factorized approach is 12x faster! I must use it. Now, where to store v_proj? Each head needs v_proj of size N*D = 2304 floats. I can store it in shared memory alongside the other arrays. Shared memory total with v_proj explicitly stored: - norm_nodes: 2304 - qkv: 6912 - attn: 576 - v_proj: 2304 - out_smem: 2304 - scratch: 256 Total: 14656 floats = 58.6 KB Still fine. But qkv is 6912 floats and v_proj is 2304. Total with norm_nodes: 2304 + 6912 + 2304 + 2304 + 576 + 256 = 14656. If I want to reduce shared memory, I can compute v_proj on the fly per (j,d) without storing the full array, and then accumulate for all n. But then I'd compute v_proj[j,d] multiple times (once per n). Or I can loop over j first, compute v_proj for a single j across all d, then accumulate `attn[n,j] * v_proj[j,d]` for all n, d. This way I only need v_proj for one j at a time. For j from 0 to N-1: 1. Compute v_proj_j[d] = sum_hd v[j,hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim+hd] for all d. This is a vector of D elements. 2. For each n: out_smem[n,d] += attn[n,j] * v_proj_j[d] If I store v_proj_j in shared memory (D elements = 96 floats), the loop is: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { // Compute v_proj_j[d] for all d - D * head_dim FMAs for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { float vp = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { vp += v_ptr[...] * out_w[...]; } v_proj_j[d] = vp; } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate for all n, d for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; out_smem[idx] += attn[n * N + j] * v_proj_j[d]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` This requires v_proj_j to be size D = 96 floats. Very small! And the inner loops are well-parallelized. For j=0..N-1 (24 iterations): - Iteration j: compute v_proj_j (96 elements) - 96 dot products of 24. With 256 threads, each thread handles a few d values. - Then accumulate into out_smem for all n, d. Actually, the accumulation can be done with 256 threads parallelizing over N*D=2304 elements. Each thread handles 2304/256 = 9 elements. For each element, it does one multiply and add. Very efficient. And for computing v_proj_j, each d requires a dot product of head_dim=24. With 256 threads doing 96 dot products, each thread does about 1/3 of a dot product. A bit underutilized, but fine. Wait, with 256 threads and D=96, `for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads)` means threads 0..95 each handle 1 d value, and threads 96..255 are idle. That's 62.5% utilization for this loop. But this loop runs 24 times (for each j). Overall, it's not too bad. Alternatively, I can compute v_proj_j with fewer threads by having each thread handle multiple d values. But the outer loop over j already parallelizes the work. Actually, we can restructure: have each thread precompute a slice of v_proj for all j and d, storing nothing, and then loop over j inside the n,d loop. No, that would be the inefficient triple loop. The j-loop approach with v_proj_j buffer is good. Let me estimate the thread utilization: For the v_proj_j computation: - 96 useful threads out of 256. ~37.5% utilization. For the accumulation: - 2304 elements / 256 = 9 iterations. All threads busy. Since the accumulation is a larger portion, overall utilization is decent. Another optimization: compute v_proj for all j at once and store in shared memory (N*D = 2304 floats). Then the accumulation is just one loop without the __syncthreads between j iterations. Memory: 2304 floats = 9.2 KB. With the current SMEM budget, adding this is fine. Let me check total SMEM with v_proj array: - norm_nodes: 2304 - qkv: 6912 - attn: 576 - v_proj: 2304 - out_smem: 2304 - scratch: 256 Total: 14656 floats = 58.6 KB This is well within the 227 KB limit. And for 4 blocks, total SMEM used by all blocks simultaneously is 4 * 58.6 = 234.4 KB. Very close to the 228 KB per SM limit! But the 4 blocks are on different SMs, so each SM only uses 58.6 KB. Fine. Wait, blocks are distributed to SMs, and each SM has 228 KB. If a block uses 58.6 KB, then each SM can hold at most floor(228 / 58.6) = 3 blocks. With 4 blocks total, most SMs run 1 block, and a few might run 2 or 3. Fine. OK, I'll use the v_proj array. But wait, I just realized: I don't need to store QKV for all heads simultaneously if I process one head at a time! This would save a lot of shared memory. Shared memory for head-by-head: - norm_nodes: 2304 - q: 576 - k: 576 - v: 576 - attn: 576 - v_proj: 2304 - out_smem: 2304 - scratch: 256 Total: 9472 floats = 37.9 KB Much less! And Q, K, V per head are computed on the fly from norm_nodes. QKV computation per head: each thread handles some (n, hd) elements. Total elements per head: 576. With 256 threads, 576/256 = ~2.25 per thread. Each is a dot product of D=96. Total FMAs per thread for QKV: ~2.25 * 3 * 96 = ~648. For all heads: 4 * 648 = ~2592 FMAs per thread. Still not a lot, but reasonable. And we save shared memory. This seems like the better approach. But wait, earlier I thought computing QKV per head on the fly means the block does 55k FMAs per head * 4 = 221k FMAs for QKV. With 256 threads, that's 221k / 256 = 864 FMAs per thread. Right, 864 FMAs per thread. That's a decent amount. Actually, it's 864 FMAs for QKV. Plus attention and output, total maybe 2k-3k FMAs per thread. With 67 TFLOPS peak, minimum time is in the hundreds of nanoseconds. But with memory stalls and syncs, probably microseconds. OK, I'll use the head-by-head approach with less shared memory. Here's the final kernel design: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ norm_nodes_in, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ q_w, const float* __restrict__ k_w, const float* __restrict__ v_w, const float* __restrict__ rel_w1, const float* __restrict__ rel_b1, const float* __restrict__ rel_w2, const float* __restrict__ rel_b2, const float* __restrict__ out_w, float* __restrict__ out_acc, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, int head_dim, float scale ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* norm_nodes = smem; // N * D float* q = norm_nodes + N * D; // N * head_dim float* k = q + N * head_dim; // N * head_dim float* v = k + N * head_dim; // N * head_dim float* attn = v + N * head_dim; // N * N float* v_proj = attn + N * N; // N * D float* out_smem = v_proj + N * D; // N * D float* scratch = out_smem + N * D; // blockDim.x // Load normalized nodes for this batch for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { norm_nodes[idx] = norm_nodes_in[b * N * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Initialize output accumulator in shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { out_smem[idx] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Process each head for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { // Compute Q, K, V for this head int qkv_size = N * head_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < qkv_size; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; int w_col = h * head_dim + hd; float qv = 0, kv = 0, vv = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float nd = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; qv += nd * q_w[w_col * D + d]; kv += nd * k_w[w_col * D + d]; vv += nd * v_w[w_col * D + d]; } q[idx] = qv; k[idx] = kv; v[idx] = vv; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores + rel_bias for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { score += q[i * head_dim + hd] * k[j * head_dim + hd]; } score *= scale; // rel_mlp float pf[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { pf[p] = pair_feats[b * N * N * P + i * N * P + j * P + p]; } float tmp[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { float val = rel_b1[p]; for (int pp = 0; pp < P; pp++) { val += pf[pp] * rel_w1[p * P + pp]; } val = val * sigmoid_fast(val); tmp[p] = val; } float bias = rel_b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { bias += tmp[p] * rel_w2[h * P + p]; } score += bias; if (edge_mask[b * N * N + i * N + j] < 0.5f) { score = -1e9f; } attn[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i * N + j]); } max_val = block_reduce_max(max_val, scratch); float exp_sum = 0; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { float e = expf(attn[i * N + j] - max_val); attn[i * N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } exp_sum = block_reduce_sum(exp_sum, scratch); for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { attn[i * N + j] /= exp_sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Compute v_proj[j, d] = sum_hd v[j, hd] * out_w[d, h*head_dim + hd] for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int j = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float vp = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { vp += v[j * head_dim + hd] * out_w[d * D + h * head_dim + hd]; } v_proj[idx] = vp; } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate: out_smem[n, d] += sum_j attn[n, j] * v_proj[j, d] for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float accum = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { accum += attn[n * N + j] * v_proj[j * D + d]; } out_smem[idx] += accum; } __syncthreads(); } // Write to global memory for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { out_acc[b * N * D + idx] = out_smem[idx]; } } ``` Shared memory: - norm_nodes: 2304 - q: 576 - k: 576 - v: 576 - attn: 576 - v_proj: 2304 - out_smem: 2304 - scratch: 256 Total: 9472 floats = 37.9 KB Good. And for the residual add, I'll write a tiny kernel: ```cpp __global__ void add_residual_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ out_acc, float* __restrict__ output, int total_elements ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total_elements) return; output[idx] = nodes[idx] + out_acc[idx]; } ``` And in Python: ```python out_acc = torch.empty(B, N, D, device=nodes.device, dtype=nodes.dtype) # ... launch fused_kernel ... output = torch.empty_like(nodes) # ... launch add_residual_kernel ... return output ``` Actually, `out_acc` can be initialized and then used directly. But the kernel writes to all elements, so no initialization needed. Wait, I need to make sure the `nodes` passed to `add_residual_kernel` is the original `nodes`, and `out_acc` is what the fused kernel wrote. Let me finalize the code. But first, I should consider whether there's a way to avoid the second kernel entirely by passing `nodes` to the fused kernel and doing the add there. If I load `nodes` in the fused kernel and add it to `out_smem` before writing, I can write the final output directly. In the fused kernel: ```cpp // Before writing, add residual for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { out_acc[b * N * D + idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx] + out_smem[idx]; } ``` This saves a second kernel launch! And since `nodes` is already in cache (we just read `norm_nodes` from it), it should be efficient. Wait, in my design, `norm_nodes_in` is already the result of LayerNorm, not the original nodes. To add the residual, I need the original `nodes`. So I should pass both `norm_nodes_in` and `nodes` to the kernel. Actually, yes: in `ModelNew.forward`: ```python h = self.norm(nodes) # launch fused_kernel with h and nodes ``` And in the kernel, at the end: ```cpp // Write final output = original_nodes + out_proj(context) for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { output[b * N * D + idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx] + out_smem[idx]; } ``` This eliminates the second kernel entirely! So the final design is: 1. PyTorch LayerNorm 2. Custom fused kernel (attention + output proj + residual) 2 kernels total. Let me rewrite the kernel signature: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ norm_nodes_in, // LayerNorm output (B, N, D) const float* __restrict__ orig_nodes, // Original nodes for residual (B, N, D) const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, ... weights ..., float* __restrict__ output, // Final output (B, N, D) ... ) ``` This is great. One custom kernel, plus PyTorch LayerNorm. Actually, I can also integrate LayerNorm into the custom kernel to reduce to ONE total kernel. But PyTorch's LayerNorm is very efficient, and integrating it adds complexity. Plus we only save one kernel launch. Hmm, but one kernel launch for LayerNorm plus one custom kernel = 2 kernels. With PyTorch reference having ~12, that's still a 6x reduction. Actually wait, the PyTorch LayerNorm kernel is the first of ~12. I already cut it down to 2 (LayerNorm + custom). That's good enough. But if I want to be even faster, I can integrate LayerNorm. Each block would load `nodes`, compute LayerNorm, store in shared memory, and then proceed. This saves writing/reading the normalized nodes. Memory traffic with PyTorch LayerNorm: - LayerNorm reads nodes (36 KB), writes h (36 KB) - Custom kernel reads h (36 KB), writes output (36 KB) - Plus pair_feats, edge_mask, etc. Total for nodes/h: 144 KB (3 reads, 1 write of 36KB each) With integrated LayerNorm: - Custom kernel reads nodes (36 KB), writes output (36 KB) - pair_feats, edge_mask, weights Total for nodes: 72 KB (1 read, 1 write) So integrated LayerNorm saves 72 KB of memory traffic. For a workload this small, maybe it doesn't matter much. But it also saves a kernel launch, which might matter. The cost: each of the 4 blocks computes LayerNorm for its batch. With N=24 and D=96, LayerNorm is not expensive. And since a block handles one batch, there's no redundancy. Let me integrate LayerNorm to make it fully fused! LayerNorm computation per batch: For each node n: 1. Compute mean: sum_d nodes[n, d] / D 2. Compute variance: sum_d (nodes[n, d] - mean)^2 / D 3. Normalize: (nodes[n, d] - mean) / sqrt(var + 1e-5) * weight[d] + bias[d] This is done in shared memory. Here's the updated kernel: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, // (B, N, D) const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ norm_w, const float* __restrict__ norm_b, const float* __restrict__ q_w, const float* __restrict__ k_w, const float* __restrict__ v_w, const float* __restrict__ rel_w1, const float* __restrict__ rel_b1, const float* __restrict__ rel_w2, const float* __restrict__ rel_b2, const float* __restrict__ out_w, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, int head_dim, float scale ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* norm_nodes = smem; // N * D float* q = norm_nodes + N * D; // N * head_dim float* k = q + N * head_dim; // N * head_dim float* v = k + N * head_dim; // N * head_dim float* attn = v + N * head_dim; // N * N float* v_proj = attn + N * N; // N * D float* out_smem = v_proj + N * D; // N * D float* scratch = out_smem + N * D; // blockDim.x float* reduction_smem = scratch + num_threads; // N * 2 (for mean/var reduction) // Load nodes and compute LayerNorm for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { norm_nodes[idx] = nodes[b * N * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm per node for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { float sum = 0.0f, sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { float val = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; sum += val; sq_sum += val * val; } sum = block_reduce_sum(sum, scratch); sq_sum = block_reduce_sum(sq_sum, scratch); float mean = sum / D; float var = sq_sum / D - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { float val = norm_nodes[n * D + d]; norm_nodes[n * D + d] = (val - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } __syncthreads(); } // Initialize output accumulator for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { out_smem[idx] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Then proceed with attention as before... } ``` Wait, the LayerNorm requires `block_reduce_sum` twice per node (for sum and sq_sum). For N=24 nodes, that's 48 block reductions. With `__syncthreads()` in each reduction, that's 48 * ~9 = ~432 syncs per block. That's a lot of syncs, but each sync is cheap. Actually, I can optimize LayerNorm by having threads compute both sum and sq_sum in one pass, then do two reductions. Still 24 passes and 48 reductions. Since D=96 and block_size=256, only 96 threads participate in the sum loop. The rest are idle but participate in the reduction. This is somewhat wasteful. Alternatively, I can have each thread handle one node, computing the full mean/var for that node. Then no syncs are needed for LayerNorm! With 256 threads and N=24 nodes, I can assign roughly 10 threads per node. Or simpler: have 24 threads handle one node each, and the remaining 232 threads are idle. But 24 < 32 (warp size), so one warp handles 24 nodes, each thread doing one node. The other 7 warps are completely idle for LayerNorm. That's very wasteful. Or: each thread handles multiple nodes. 256 threads / 24 nodes = ~10.7 threads per node. Each thread handles a subset of D=96 elements for its assigned node, then a small reduction within the node group. This gets complicated. Maybe using PyTorch LayerNorm is cleaner and not much slower for this small size. Let me stick with PyTorch LayerNorm for simplicity. Actually, I just realized: if I use 4 blocks of 64 threads each, and assign one warp (32 threads) per node for LayerNorm... no, still complicated. Let me just use PyTorch for LayerNorm. It's good enough. Actually, wait. Let me consider yet another design: use 4 blocks, each with D=96 threads. Each block handles one batch. With 96 threads: - LayerNorm: each node gets 96/24 = 4 threads. Actually, D=96 for each of N=24 nodes. - For node n, threads handle the D elements. But if I only have 96 threads, I could assign 4 threads per node (96/24 = 4). Each of 4 threads handles 24 elements of a node. Then I need a reduction of 4 threads. - Or assign 1 thread per dimension across all nodes. Not straightforward. Alternatively, block size = N = 24 threads. Each thread handles one node, computing the full mean, var, and normalized values over D=96 elements. No sync needed for LayerNorm! With 24 threads per block, N=24 nodes, each thread handles exactly one node. For QKV: each thread computes all (h, hd) elements for its node? That would be H*head_dim = 96 elements per node, each a dot of D=96. That's 96*96 = 9216 FMAs per thread for Q. A lot of work per thread. Total 24 threads * 9216 FMAs = 221k FMAs per block. Good. For attention: each thread handles one query row. For query i, thread i computes all N=24 scores (dots of head_dim=24), does softmax over 24 elements, then computes context for all D=96 elements. Wait, if each thread handles one query, it computes: 1. Attn scores for all j: 24 dots of 24 = 576 FMAs 2. rel_mlp for 24 edges: 24 * small MLP 3. Softmax over 24 values: sequential on one thread 4. Context = attn @ v: 24 dots of 24 dims for each hd? No, context is (head_dim,), so 24 dots of 24 = 576 FMAs. 5. Output projection partial: context (head_dim) @ out_w[:, :] = 24 dots of D=96 dims each = 2304 FMAs. Wait, the output projection should be done after accumulating all heads. Or each thread can accumulate its partial output across heads. With block size = 24 threads (one per query), and 4 heads: For each head h: - Thread i computes score[i, j] for j=0..N-1 - 576 FMAs - Computes 24 rel_mlps - Softmax - sequential - Computes context[i, :] - 576 FMAs - Accumulates into out_smem[i, :] += context[i, :] @ out_w[h*head_dim:(h+1)*head_dim, :] - 2304 FMAs Total per head per thread: ~3500 FMAs. For 4 heads: ~14k FMAs. Total per block: 24 * 14k = 336k FMAs. This is actually very efficient in terms of thread utilization! Each thread has a lot of sequential work, which is great for avoiding syncs. And the block has only 24 threads, so no wasted threads in the softmax (each thread handles exactly one row). But with only 24 threads per block and 4 blocks, total threads = 96. This is very few for an H100. The GPU would be severely underutilized. Each SM can run up to ~2048 threads, but we only have 96 total. That's about 5% occupancy at best. Hmm, but if each thread has 14k FMAs, and the H100 can do about 2000 FMAs per cycle per SM (?), the runtime per thread is ~7 cycles. But with instruction latency and memory stalls, maybe thousands of cycles. Actually, the H100 can issue many instructions per cycle per warp, but with only 3 warps (24 threads = 1 warp on some arch, actually 24 threads is not a full warp). Wait, a warp is 32 threads. 24 threads per block means the block has 1 warp (with 8 threads masked/tailing). Actually, CUDA still executes it as a warp of 32, but only 24 threads have valid work. The other 8 are "inactive" but still consume resources. So effectively, 4 blocks * 1 warp = 4 warps total. With ~132 SMs, each SM runs at most 1 warp. That's extremely poor utilization. So block size of 24 is not good for GPU utilization. Let me try a block size of 128. For LayerNorm, it's wasteful. For softmax, it's somewhat wasteful. But for compute-heavy parts, it's good. Actually, let me just go with 256 threads and accept the softmax inefficiency. The key metric is end-to-end time, and fusing everything will help a lot. OK, final decision: - PyTorch LayerNorm - Custom fused kernel with 4 blocks (one per batch), 256 threads each - The custom kernel handles QKV per head, attention, output projection, and residual add Wait, I realize I should also consider using 4 blocks with 128 threads. The compute-heavy parts are more balanced, and softmax reductions have better utilization (128 threads for 24 elements - first reduction step only 64 threads useful, then 32, then 16, 8, 4, 2, 1 - about 7 steps). With 64 threads: 32 useful in first step, then 16, 8, 4, 2, 1 - 6 steps. With 256 threads: 128, 64, 32, 16, 8, 4, 2, 1 - 8 steps. But 232 threads are idle for the first step. Actually, for a tree reduction, the number of steps is log2(block_size). The total work is proportional to block_size (each thread writes to shared mem). So smaller block sizes have fewer steps but also fewer total threads for the compute-heavy parts. I think 128 threads is a good middle ground. Let me use 128. Actually, let me just test with 256 first and see. I can change it later. One more concern: `load_inline` compilation time. I should make the CUDA code as small and clean as possible to compile quickly. Let me write the complete Python module and test compilation. Actually, I realize that for the `reduce_and_residual_kernel` (if I use it), or for the atomic approach, the code is simpler. Let me reconsider. Atomic approach: - 16 blocks, one per (batch, head) - Each block computes attention + context, then does output projection and atomic-add to output buffer - Then a simple element-wise kernel adds residual 16 blocks is much better for GPU utilization than 4 blocks! And the atomic-add contention is minimal (4 threads write to each output element on average). Let me estimate performance: - Kernel 1: 16 blocks * 256 threads = 4096 total threads - Kernel 2: 4 * 96 / 256 = ~2 blocks * 256 threads = 512 threads Total threads: 4608. Still not great for 132 SMs, but better. Memory traffic: - LayerNorm: read nodes (36KB), write h (36KB) - Kernel 1: 16 blocks each read h (36KB * 16? No - only 4 batches, 4 blocks per batch read the same h). So total h reads: 4*36KB = 144KB. Pair_feats: 4*147KB = 588KB. Edge_mask: 4*9KB = 36KB. Output buffer atomics: 4*36KB = 144KB (read-modify-write). - Kernel 2: read output buffer (36KB), read nodes (36KB), write output (36KB) Total: ~1 MB. Wait, each of the 4 blocks per batch reads the same h. But when they run on different SMs, the L2 cache might serve the second, third, and fourth reads if the first block's read is still in cache. Since h is only 36 KB, it easily fits in L2. So the actual memory bandwidth might not be 4x. Similarly for pair_feats and edge_mask. So the 16-block approach might not be that bad for memory traffic in practice. And it has much better parallelism. With 16 blocks, we can keep up to 16 SMs busy simultaneously. I'll go with the 16-block atomic approach. The code is also simpler (no v_proj array, no accumulation across heads in shared memory). Final kernel for 16-block atomic approach: ```cpp __global__ void fused_attention_head_kernel( const float* __restrict__ norm_nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ q_w, const float* __restrict__ k_w, const float* __restrict__ v_w, const float* __restrict__ rel_w1, const float* __restrict__ rel_b1, const float* __restrict__ rel_w2, const float* __restrict__ rel_b2, const float* __restrict__ out_w, float* __restrict__ out_acc, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, int head_dim, float scale ) { int b = blockIdx.x / H; int h = blockIdx.x % H; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* q = smem; float* k = q + N * head_dim; float* v = k + N * head_dim; float* attn = v + N * head_dim; float* scratch = attn + N * N; float* norm_buf = scratch + num_threads; // Load norm_nodes for this batch for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { norm_buf[idx] = norm_nodes[b * N * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q, K, V for this head int qkv_size = N * head_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < qkv_size; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; int w_col = h * head_dim + hd; float qv = 0, kv = 0, vv = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float nd = norm_buf[n * D + d]; qv += nd * q_w[w_col * D + d]; kv += nd * k_w[w_col * D + d]; vv += nd * v_w[w_col * D + d]; } q[idx] = qv; k[idx] = kv; v[idx] = vv; } __syncthreads(); // Attention scores for (int idx = tid; idx < N * N; idx += num_threads) { int i = idx / N; int j = idx % N; float score = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { score += q[i * head_dim + hd] * k[j * head_dim + hd]; } score *= scale; // rel_mlp float pf[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { pf[p] = pair_feats[b * N * N * P + i * N * P + j * P + p]; } float tmp[16]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { float val = rel_b1[p]; for (int pp = 0; pp < P; pp++) { val += pf[pp] * rel_w1[p * P + pp]; } // SiLU float s = val; val = s / (1.0f + expf(-s)); val = s * val; tmp[p] = val; } float bias = rel_b2[h]; for (int p = 0; p < P; p++) { bias += tmp[p] * rel_w2[h * P + p]; } score += bias; if (edge_mask[b * N * N + i * N + j] < 0.5f) { score = -1e9f; } attn[idx] = score; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, attn[i * N + j]); } max_val = block_reduce_max(max_val, scratch); float exp_sum = 0; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { float e = expf(attn[i * N + j] - max_val); attn[i * N + j] = e; exp_sum += e; } exp_sum = block_reduce_sum(exp_sum, scratch); for (int j = tid; j < N; j += num_threads) { attn[i * N + j] /= exp_sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Context + output projection partial, atomic add for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float ctx = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { float c = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { c += attn[n * N + j] * v[j * head_dim + hd]; } ctx += c * out_w[d * D + h * head_dim + hd]; } atomicAdd(&out_acc[b * N * D + idx], ctx); } } ``` Wait, the context computation can be optimized: `partial[n, d] = sum_hd [sum_j attn[n,j] * v[j,hd]] * out_w[d, h*head_dim+hd]` I can compute `context[n, hd] = sum_j attn[n,j] * v[j,hd]` first, then dot with out_w. ```cpp // Compute context into q (reuse) for (int idx = tid; idx < qkv_size; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / head_dim; int hd = idx % head_dim; float c = 0; for (int j = 0; j < N; j++) { c += attn[n * N + j] * v[j * head_dim + hd]; } q[idx] = c; // reuse q as context buffer } __syncthreads(); // Output projection partial for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += num_threads) { int n = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float partial = 0; for (int hd = 0; hd < head_dim; hd++) { partial += q[n * head_dim + hd] * out_w[d * D + h * head_dim + hd]; } atomicAdd(&out_acc[b * N * D + idx], partial); } ``` This is cleaner: first compute context (N*head_dim elements), then output projection (N*D elements). The context is stored in shared memory (reusing q). Total shared memory: - q (context): N*head_dim = 576 - k: 576 (unused after attention, but we keep it) - v: 576 - attn: 576 - scratch: 256 - norm_buf: N*D = 2304 Total: 4864 floats = 19.5 KB Wait, k is unused after the attention scores are computed. But since we're reusing q for context, and k is just sitting there, that's fine. We don't need to reclaim it. Actually, if we stored norm_buf at the beginning of smem, then q, k, v, attn, scratch after it: - norm_buf: 2304 - q: 576 - k: 576 - v: 576 - attn: 576 - scratch: 256 Total: 4864 floats = 19.5 KB This is very compact. OK, I'm going with this 16-block atomic approach. Then a simple element-wise add kernel for residual. Combined, the code is simpler and should have good performance. return e / (1.0f + e);\n }\n}\n\n__device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val, float* shared) {\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n shared[tid] = val;\n __syncthreads();\n for (int s = blockDim.x >> 1; s > 0; s >>= 1) {\n if (tid < s) {\n shared[tid] += shared[tid + s];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n return shared[0];\n}\n\n__device__ float block_reduce_max(float val, float* shared) {\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n shared[tid] = val;\n __syncthreads();\n for (int s = blockDim.x >> 1; s > 0; s >>= 1) {\n if (tid < s) {\n shared[tid] = fmaxf(shared[tid], shared[tid + s]);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n nodes,\n torch::Tensor out_acc,\n torch::Tensor output,\n int total_elements\n) {\n int block_size = 256;\n int grid_size = (total_elements + block_size - 1) / block_size;\n add_residual_kernel<<<grid_size, block_size>>>(\n nodes.data_ptr<float>(),\n out_acc.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n total_elements\n );\n}\n'''\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def
